Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/asset/images/medium/nn1c00131_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/asset/images/large/nn1c00131_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.1c00131&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Zinc sulfide (ZnS) exhibits promise in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) because of its low operation voltage and high theoretical specific capacity. However, pristine ZnS is not adequate in realizing rapid and robust sodium storage owing to its low reversibility, poor structure stability, and sluggish kinetics. To date, most efforts focus on utilizing carbonaceous incorporation to improve its electrochemical performances. Nevertheless, it remains an arduous challenge for realizing superior rate capability while obtaining stable cycling. Herein, inspired by the crystal structure of hexagonal ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, which possesses an intrinsic layered feature with larger unit-cell volume versus that of ZnS, indium incorporation is thus deployed as an immediate remedy. <i>In/ex situ</i> investigations combined with density functional theory calculations are conducted to reveal the superior kinetics, high reversibility, and good structure stability of ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>. Notably, the formed indium-based derivatives during cycling manifest a Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation process, thereby exciting a synergetic mechanism to stabilize electrochemical cycling. As a result, the electrochemical performances of Zn-based sulfide are significantly improved via the indium incorporation. Furthermore, a full cell based on the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> anode with the superior electrochemical performance is developed. This work provides an effective tactic of heteroatom incorporation for optimizing structure as well as exciting a complementary reaction process toward developing superior anodes for high-performance alkali-ion batteries.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D8507%26pageCount%3D10%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DDong%2BYan%252C%2BYew%2BVon%2BLim%252C%2BGuangzhao%2BWang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D15%26issueNum%3D5%26contentID%3Dacsnano.1c00131%26title%3DUnlocking%2BRapid%2Band%2BRobust%2BSodium%2BStorage%2BPerformance%2Bof%2BZinc-Based%2BSulfide%2Bvia%2BIndium%2BIncorporation%26numPages%3D10%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1936-0851%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dancac3%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D8516%26publicationDate%3DMay%2B2021" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright Â© 2021 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Batteries" class="article__tags__link">Batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291445&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Chemical structure" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Chemical%20structure" class="article__tags__link">Chemical structure</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290830&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Diffusion" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Diffusion" class="article__tags__link">Diffusion</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290905&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Kinetics" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Kinetics" class="article__tags__link">Kinetics</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Znin2s4&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_ZnIn2S4">ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Crystal+Structure&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_crystal%20structure">crystal structure</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode+Material&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode%20material">anode material</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Reaction+Process&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_reaction%20process">reaction process</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="NLM_p first">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been extended as a prospective energy storageâconversion device owing to abundant sodium resources. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 1 to 5">(1â5)</a> However, current challenges, such as the poor reversibility, low rate performance, and short cycling life, still impede their practical applications. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 6 to 10">(6â10)</a> Developing superior electrode materials for SIBs is an essentially effective strategy and has attracted much attention. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 11 to 23">(11â23)</a> Among the various anode materials, ZnS is a highly appealing anode material due to its low operation voltage (&lt;1 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) and high theoretical specific capacity (â 581.4 mAh g<sup>â1</sup>). Nevertheless, the ZnS anode usually suffers from drastic structure changes, poor reversibility, and sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 24 to 29">(24â29)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">To date, various effective methods have been developed aiming at improving the electrochemical performances of ZnS, such as utilizing carbon incorporation, morphology design, and hybrid material construction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 24 to 31">(24â31)</a> For instance, Zhang et al. incorporated graphene with ZnS quantum dots to enhance the electrochemical cycling stability and reversibility of the ZnS, leading to an improved specific capacity of 491 mAh g<sup>â1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>â1</sup> after 100 cycles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> Fang et al. enhanced the electrochemical kinetics and structure stability of the ZnS by hybridizing with Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> and N-doped carbon nanosheet, exhibiting an enhanced specific capacity of 542 mAh g<sup>â1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>â1</sup> after 100 cycles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> Despite the encouraging results, improving the electrochemical performances of ZnS in SIBs without the carbon incorporation is rarely reported, while the incorporation of carbonaceous materials could impede its electrochemical activity. Thereby, it is still challenging to utilize the Zn-based sulfides for rapid and robust sodium storage.</div><div class="NLM_p">Recently, heteroatom incorporation has been demonstrated as an effective strategy for material optimization in SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32,33)</a> This strategy relies on fundamental structure modifications, which is essentially different from the common carbonaceous decoration approach. For example, Huang et al. incorporated cobalt to increase the unit-cell volume of iron sulfide (from 66.72 to 66.95 Ã<sup>3</sup>) and thereby improved its electrochemical kinetics. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> Nevertheless, the improvement of electrochemical performances is still limited after cobalt incorporation due to its limited effect on optimizing crystal structure. Compared with only optimizing the original crystal structure, the incorporation of heteroatoms to form a derivative crystal structure with significantly expanded unit-cell volume is more desirable. The large unit-cell volume is beneficial for not only boosting diffusion kinetics but also offering a more appropriate host structure for robust sodium storage. Among the various Zn-based sulfides, hexagonal ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> exhibits the theoretical lattice parameters of <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 3.85 Ã and <i>c</i> = 24.68 Ã (JCPDS 03-065-2023) with a large unit-cell volume (<i>V</i> = 316.81 Ã<sup>3</sup>). These are much larger than those of the conventional ZnS, such as the hexagonal ZnS (<i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 3.82 Ã; <i>c</i> = 18.74 Ã; <i>V</i> = 237.25 Ã<sup>3</sup>; JCPDS 01-089-2156) and cubic ZnS (<i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = <i>c</i> = 5.40 Ã; <i>V</i> = 157.46 Ã<sup>3</sup>; JCPDS 03-065-0309). Moreover, the hexagonal ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> also displays an intrinsic layered structure with alternatively stacking layers of SâZnâSâInâSâInâS along the <i>c</i>-axis (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34 ref35 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 34 to 36">(34â36)</a> These are in favor of constructing two-dimensional (2D) morphology to offer abundant exposed electrochemical active sites, and hence boost pseudocapacitive behavior and diffusion kinetics. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> However, comprehensive studies on ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> as SIB anodes are yet to be reported.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/asset/images/medium/nn1c00131_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/asset/images/large/nn1c00131_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Structure characterization of ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>: Crystal structure illustration (a). Rietveld refined XRD pattern (b). Raman spectrum (c) and photograph of as-prepared ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> (inset). SEM (dâf), TEM (g), HRTEM (h), and FFT pattern (inset). EDS spectrum (i) and corresponding element contents (inset). Elemental mapping images (jâm).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/asset/images/large/nn1c00131_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.1c00131&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, inspired by the crystallography, we employed the indium incorporation to develop the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> as an SIB anode. The ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> anode exhibited a high specific capacity of 545.0 mAh g<sup>â1</sup> at 50 mA g<sup>â1</sup>, prominent rate capability of 394.3 mAh g<sup>â1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>â1</sup>, and excellent cycling stability of â¼100% capacity retention during 500 cycles, which outperforms most of the previously reported Zn-based sulfides and other advanced anode materials. In-depth analyses were conducted to reveal the optimization mechanism of indium incorporation, attributing to its induced synergistic effects among the layered structure feature, superior electrochemical kinetics, good structure integrity upon cycling, and complementary reaction mechanism. Furthermore, a full cell based on the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> anode with excellent electrochemical performance was demonstrated.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">In this work, the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> was prepared through a hydrothermal reaction (details in the <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec4" aria-label="Experimental Section">Experimental Section</a>; <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>), and the ZnS was synthesized via the same process without adding the indium source. The Rietveld refined X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern of the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b) indicates reasonable <i>R</i>-factors <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> (<i>R</i><sub>p</sub> = 3.82, <i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> = 5.16, <i>R</i><sub>exp</sub> = 1.99) without impurities, confirming the hexagonal ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> with the <i>PÌ</i>3m1 space group (JCPDS 03-065-2023). Lattice parameters of the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> derived from the refinement are <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 3.85697 and <i>c</i> = 24.89847 with a cell volume of 320.772 Ã<sup>3</sup>. Refined XRD of the as-prepared ZnS was performed via employing hexagonal ZnS (JCPDS 01-089-2156; <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a</a>) and cubic ZnS (JCPDS 03-065-0309; <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2b</a>) with a reasonable fit of the profile (<i>R</i><sub>p</sub> = 2.94, <i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> = 4.00, <i>R</i><sub>exp</sub> = 1.20; <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2c</a>). Lattice parameters of the cubic ZnS from the refinement are <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = <i>c</i> = 5.40534 with the cell volume of 157.932 Ã<sup>3</sup>, and those of the hexagonal ZnS are <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 3.81768 and <i>c</i> = 18.82301 with the cell volume of 237.585 Ã<sup>3</sup>. The interlayer spacings of exposed crystal planes from the refined XRD are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Tables S1âS3</a>. As a comparison, the cell volume and interlayer spacing of the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> are larger than those of the ZnS, which are more beneficial for sodium-ion diffusion. Raman analysis was then conducted and indicates three peaks centered at 358, 294, and 248 cm<sup>â1</sup>, ascribed to longitudinal optical (LO<sub>2</sub>), transverse optical (TO<sub>2</sub>), and longitudinal optical (LO<sub>1</sub>) modes of the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> There are no signals of carbonaceous contribution (around 1300â1600 cm<sup>â1</sup>) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> and other impurities, further demonstrating the pure ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>. Morphology of the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> was examined via scanning electron microscopy (SEM; <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>dâf and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S3</a>) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM; <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>g). A microsphere with an interlaced nanosheet morphology is displayed (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>dâg and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S3</a>). The lattice interplanar distance of 0.192 nm assigned to the (110) plane of ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref41" aria-label="reference 35 and 41">(35,41)</a> was indicated through the high-resolution TEM (HRTEM; <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>h) and fast Fourier transform (FFT; inset in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>h). The energy dispersive X-ray (EDX) spectrum and the element mapping images demonstrate the existence and uniform distribution of Zn, In, and S (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>jâm). The Zn/In/S ratio is at 1:2:4 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>i) and consistent with the success in formation of the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>. This is further confirmed by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS; <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>) results. In contrast, the as-prepared ZnS shows the microspherical morphology assembled from nanoparticles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5aâc</a>). Further EDX results demonstrate the uniform distribution of Zn and S in the ZnS (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5eâg</a>) with the Zn/S ratio of 1:1 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5d</a>). Notably, the difference in the morphology between the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and ZnS is ascribed to their intrinsic structural features. The hexagonal ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> exhibits an intrinsic layered structure, which is in favor of constructing the 2D morphology to result in its higher pore volume and larger specific surface area versus those of the ZnS (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S4</a>). These can help to offer more exposed electrochemical active sites for sodium-ion transfer and storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> In general, the indium incorporation strategy can arouse the layered structure of Zn-based sulfide, hence leading to the big cell volume, large interlayer spacing, and abundant sodium-ion diffusion channels for ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> to realize its boosted electrochemical kinetics.</div><div class="NLM_p">Cyclic voltammetry (CV) of the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> was conducted at 0.1 mV s<sup>â1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>). In the first cathodic scan, three cathodic peaks at 1, 0.6, and 0.3 V corresponding to sodiation process and formation of a solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film are shown. In the corresponding anodic scan, peaks at 0.45, 0.6, 0.9, and 1.2 V indicate a stepwise desodiation process. Referring to the mechanism discussion as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>, the cathodicâanodic peaks of CV correspond well to the working process (details in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>). Subsequent cycles demonstrate excellent electrochemical reversibility with overall similarity of CV profiles, indicating the stable kinetics and low polarization of the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>. Galvanostatic chargeâdischarge profiles show that ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> exhibits initial discharge and charge capacities of 894.4 and 562.4 mAh g<sup>â1</sup>, respectively, with the Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 62.8% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a). The initial irreversible loss is attributed to the well-designed open layered structure of the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, thereby resulting in the large irreversible reactions occurring at the interface for the SEI formation during the first sodiation process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33 ref42 ref43 ref44 ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref42 ref43 ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 33 and 42 to 45">(33,42â45)</a> With cycling, the CE of ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> increases to 94% in the second cycle and gradually to 98% within 4 cycles, much higher than those of the ZnS (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S8</a>). This is direct proof of the boosted electrochemical reversibility with indium incorporation. After 50 cycles, the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> shows a reversible specific capacity of 545.0 mAh g<sup>â1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b), showing a much better capacity retention (96.9%) than the ZnS (50.2%). Notably, the reversible capacity of as-prepared ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> is slightly larger than its theoretical specific capacity (â538.3 mAh g<sup>â1</sup>) mainly induced by the interfacial charge storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46 ref47 ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 46 to 48">(46â48)</a> Conventionally during sodiation, the alkali-ion is first intercalated into the bulk structure of metal-compound anodes, followed by converting to phase-separated alkali-ion compounds and metal. Further anomalies with capacitive features often occur at the interfaces between the ionic compounds and metal within the low potential to offer additional capacities. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46 ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 46 and 47">(46,47)</a> In addition, the unconventional electrochemical activity of defects at the interfaces and the amorphous phase area may contribute to additional capacities especially for anodes with pseudocapacitive behavior. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46 ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref48" aria-label="reference 46 and 48">(46,48)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c further compares the rate performances of ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and ZnS. ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> exhibits much improved rate capability and more stable CE in stark contrast to the ZnS, delivering reversible capacities of 566.7, 559.7, 554.9, 523.4, 500.8, 473.2, and 394.3 mAh g<sup>â1</sup> at 50, 100, 200, 500, 1000, 2000, and 5000 mA g<sup>â1</sup>, respectively. As the cycling rate returns to 50 mA g<sup>â1</sup>, the reversible capacity of ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> reverts to 556.5 mAh g<sup>â1</sup>, demonstrating the good stability. Overall, the electrochemical performance of ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> is more excellent than those of other reported Zn-based anode materials (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d) and other advanced anode materials in SIBs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S5</a>), especially on the rate performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref27 ref29 ref31 ref49 ref50 ref51 ref52 ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref27 ref29 ref31 ref49 ref50 ref51 ref52 ref53" aria-label="reference 25, 27, 29, 31 and 49 to 53">(25,27,29,31,49â53)</a> In addition, the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> also exhibits much improved capacity retention of â¼100% within 500 cycles versus the ZnS (â¼10%; <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e). In view of the fact that cycling fading is usually due to the lost in structural intactness of the electrode materials ascribed to their fatigue and pulverization, <i>ex situ</i> SEM measurements were further employed to investigate structure stability of both electrode materials after 500 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S9 and S10</a>). The ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> almost maintains its overall spherelike morphology, and the layered structure is covered by the generated SEI film during repeated (de)sodiation processes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>). No obvious signals of collapse and pulverization occur in the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, while the microsphere morphology of ZnS completely collapses (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>). The results further demonstrate the stable structure of ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> during electrochemical cycling mainly due to its open-layered structure with abundant exposed active sites, large interlayer spacing, and big cell volume induced by the indium incorporation. These structural features are beneficial for alleviating the mechanical strain and prompting stable Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, which is further supported by the investigation of improvement mechanisms including the kinetics analysis and reaction mechanism in later sections (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>â<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>). To further evaluate the applicable performances of ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> in SIBs, a full SIB was assembled by employing the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> as an anode and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C as a cathode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure. </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f). Besides, we employed the presodiation technology to reduce the irreversible sodium loss when we assembled the full cell (detailed in the <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec4" aria-label="Experimental Section">Experimental Section</a>). The electrochemical performances of the full cell (based on mass loading of cathode materials) are depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>g and h, indicating a reversible capacity of 88.4 mAh g<sup>â1</sup> at 200 mA g<sup>â1</sup> after 100 cycles with capacity retention of 94% and CE of 99%. As calculated (details in the <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec4" aria-label="Experimental Section">Experimental Section</a>), the full cell exhibits a high energy density of 294 Wh kg<sup>â1</sup> at a power density of 333 W kg<sup>â1</sup> (based on the total mass of active materials; <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>g). Notably, a gradual capacity increment is observed during initial cycles for the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> based half and full cells (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b, c, e, and h), which is more obvious at the high current density (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e). This is usually attributed to the activation process of active materials during the initial cycles, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> and the cell needs more cycles to be fully awakened at the high current densities with fast operation rate.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/asset/images/medium/nn1c00131_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/asset/images/large/nn1c00131_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Electrochemical performances: Chargeâdischarge curves of ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> at various cycles (a). Cycling capability at 50 mA g<sup>â1</sup> (b). Rate property (c). Comparison of the rate performance with various reported Zn-based SIB anodes (d). Cycling capability at 2 A g<sup>â1</sup> (e). Chargeâdischarge profiles of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C cathode and ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> anode at the 50th cycle (f). Chargeâdischarge profile of full battery at the 50th cycle (based on mass loading of cathode material) (g). Cycling capability of full battery at 200 mA g<sup>â1</sup> (h).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/asset/images/large/nn1c00131_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.1c00131&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/asset/images/medium/nn1c00131_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/asset/images/large/nn1c00131_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Surface kinetic behaviors of ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>: Side (a) and top (b) views of the charge density difference for the Na<sup>+</sup> adsorbed onto the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> surface. The yellow and cyan regions indicate the charge accumulation and depletion, respectively. CV curves conducted at the different scan rates (c). Regression relationship between log(scan rate) and log(peak current) (d). Capacitive and diffusion contributions of the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> at 1.0 mV s<sup>â1</sup> (e). Normalized ratio of diffusion and capacitance at the different scan rates (f).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/asset/images/large/nn1c00131_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.1c00131&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The surface kinetic behavior of ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> was first studied by density functional theory (DFT) calculation and pseudocapacitive analysis to ascertain the factors contributing to the electrochemical performance. The charge density differences for Na<sup>+</sup> adsorbed on the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and ZnS surfaces were modeled and are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>aâb and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S11a, b, e, and f</a>. Charge redistribution is observed after Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption with strong electron depletion around the adsorbed Na region. The ionic adsorption behavior is modeled after the following expression:<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/asset/images/medium/nn1c00131_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i3"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span>where <i>E</i><sub>total</sub> and <i>E</i><sub>ZnIn2S4/ZnS</sub> are the total energies of ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and ZnS surfaces with and without the Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption, respectively. <i>E</i><sub>Na</sub> is the energy inclined to Na self-clustering. The ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and ZnS exhibit the <i>E</i><sub>adsorption</sub> around â1.89 and â2.78 eV, respectively, and the negative value of <i>E</i><sub>adsorption</sub> indicates energetically favorable Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption on the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and ZnS surfaces instead of self-clustering. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55 ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55 ref56" aria-label="reference 55 and 56">(55,56)</a> To further analyze the surface kinetic behavior upon electrochemical evolution, CV profiles of the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> at the different scan rates were examined (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c). The relationship of the peak currents vs various scan rates obeys the equation of <i>i</i> = <i>av</i><sup><i>b</i></sup>. The terms <i>a</i>, <i>i</i>, and <i>v</i> indicate the constants, current, and scan rate, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> The electrochemical kinetic behavior at the (de)sodiation processes is identified through the <i>b</i>-value (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> The <i>b</i>-value of the anodic peak at 0.95 V is evaluated as 0.56, indicating domination by diffusion processes, and the others are mainly controlled by pseudocapacitive reaction (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d and e). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57 ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57 ref58" aria-label="reference 57 and 58">(57,58)</a> Pseudocapacitance (<i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i>) to total capacity contribution (<i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> + <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>) of the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> was further obtained via the equation of <i>i</i>(V) = <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> + <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" aria-label="reference 59">(59)</a> The pseudocapacitive contributions of ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> are calculated as 78.7, 80.6, 82.2, 83.9 and 86.0% at 0.2, 0.4, 0.6, 0.8, and 1 mV s<sup>â1</sup>, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f). The large pseudocapacitive contribution enables fast and stable Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, as well as benefits the superior rate property and long-term cycling. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> In addition, the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> shows high pseudocapacitive contributions even at low scan rates, indicating the intrinsic pseudocapacitive behavior of the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> as SIB anodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref60'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60" aria-label="reference 60">(60)</a> The intrinsic pseudocapacitive behavior favors prompting the reactions on ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> surfaces, which may awaken the defects with unconventional electrochemical activity to deliver additional charge storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46 ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref48" aria-label="reference 46 and 48">(46,48)</a> In order to confirm whether the generation of SEI layer influences the pseudocapacitive behavior during cycling, we further detected the variation of <i>b</i>-value for ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> after 100 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>). As seen, the <i>b</i>-values do not vary drastically, thereby implying the stable pseudocapacitive behavior of ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further investigate the diffusion behavior in terms of the diffusion energy barrier and overall kinetic evolution, DFT calculation and <i>in/ex situ</i> electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) were then conducted (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>). The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion processes along the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and ZnS surfaces were evaluated and are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>aâd and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S11c, d, g, and h</a>. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barriers along the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>-I, ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>-II, ZnS-hexagonal, and ZnS-cubic surfaces are 0.23, 0.19, 0.22, and 0.21 eV (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e), respectively. They manifest comparable Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barriers, and the diffusion barrier along the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>-II surface displays the lowest value with the best diffusion kinetic. Besides, their Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barriers are lower than those of other reported anode materials, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56 ref59 ref61 ref62 ref63'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56 ref59 ref61 ref62 ref63" aria-label="reference 56, 59 and 61 to 63">(56,59,61â63)</a> implying the excellent Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics on ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and ZnS surfaces. Furthermore, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients derived from the DFT simulation are displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S6</a>. It is compared that the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient on the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>-II is higher than that on the ZnS and ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>-I, further demonstrating the excellent surface kinetics on ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>-II. To further conduct in-depth kinetic investigations, <i>in situ</i> EIS measurement was then carried out at various charge/discharge states of both electrode materials after adequate activation processes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f and g). Kinetic evolutions of the two electrodes manifest almost similar trends within the 10th cycle. When sodiation from open-circuit voltage (OCV) state to 0.01 V, both the electrodes show an increase of resistances owing to the growth of SEI film induced by gradual volume expansion and the formation of insulating Na<sub>2</sub>S due to conversion processes (proven in later sections). Charging from 0.01 to 2.5 V, gradual decrease in resistances is ascribed to the decomposition of insulating Na<sub>2</sub>S and part of the SEI film. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> In stark contrast, the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> electrode demonstrates much lower resistances and subtler changes as compared to ZnS within the whole cycle, strongly supporting the superior reaction kinetics and excellent stability of the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>. In addition, <i>ex situ</i> EIS results of the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>) at various cycles exhibits small and similar resistances, while those of the ZnS (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>) increase significantly with large values. These results further reveal the more stable charge transfer kinetics of ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> in stark contrast to ZnS.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/asset/images/medium/nn1c00131_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/asset/images/large/nn1c00131_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Diffusion behaviors: Side (a and c) and top (b and d) views of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path on ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>-I and ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>-II (ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>-I means the surface with Zn and S atoms exposed at the terminal; ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>-II indicates the surface with In and S atoms exposed at the terminal). Energy barrier diffusion profiles of Na<sup>+</sup> on ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and ZnS (e). <i>In situ</i> EIS spectra of ZnS (f) and ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> (g) within the 10th cycle.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/asset/images/large/nn1c00131_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.1c00131&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further reveal the origin of the excellent electrochemical performances, it is necessary to unveil the reaction mechanisms of ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>). <i>In situ</i> XRD was conducted at 100 mA g<sup>â1</sup> within initial two cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S15</a>). The strong peaks labeled as âââ are ascribed to the signals of current collector and X-ray transmission window in the <i>in situ</i> setup. At the OCV state, patterns at â¼21.5Â°, 27.7Â°, and 47.2Â° correspond to (006), (102), and (110) planes of the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> (JCPDS No. 03-065-2023), respectively. Discharging from OCV to 0.9 V (Stage I), the peaks of ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> gradually move to the low angles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a>) due to the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation and the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>âZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> formation. With the sustained sodiation (Stage II), the peaks of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>âZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> gradually disappear, instead by the emerging peaks at â¼23.5Â° and 38.8Â° ascribed to the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>S (JCPDS 00-003-0933) and Na<sub><i>y</i></sub>âIn<sub>6</sub>S<sub>7</sub> (JCPDS 01-080-0126), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a> respectively, indicating the commencement of the conversion process. Notably, no obvious signals of Zn can be identified by the XRD mainly due to either the amorphous nature or small crystallite grains of the formed Zn. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33 ref64'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref64" aria-label="reference 33 and 64">(33,64)</a> With continuous discharging, additional phases around 20.4Â° and 35.6Â° are formed and attributed to the emergence and dominance of alloying reactions indicated by the formation of NaZn<sub>13</sub> (JCPDS No. 00-003-1008). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> At the same voltage range, the Na<sub><i>y</i></sub>âIn<sub>6</sub>S<sub>7</sub> phase at 38.8Â° is gradually shifted to the lower Bragg angle (38.0Â°; Stage III) and eventually increased to 39.6Â° (In<sub>6</sub>S<sub>7</sub>; JCPDS No. 01-080-0126) at the following desodiation reaction (Stage IV). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a> This strongly suggests the reversible Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation process. In addition, the phases of the Na<sub>2</sub>S, NaZn<sub>13</sub>, and Na<sub><i>y</i></sub>âIn<sub>6</sub>S<sub>7</sub> disappear during the succeeding desodiation process, suggesting the reversible conversion reaction and the reappearance of Na<sub><i>b</i></sub>âZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> (21.5Â°, 27.7Â°, and 47.2Â°). However, the weak XRD intensity of the reformed Na<sub><i>b</i></sub>âZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> indicates its small crystal size or poor crystallinity, which is similar to the most relevant reports of conversion-dominated anodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33 ref65'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref65" aria-label="reference 33 and 65">(33,65)</a> Moreover, the similar working process is observed within the second cycle, thus demonstrating the stable and reversible working processes.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/asset/images/medium/nn1c00131_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/asset/images/large/nn1c00131_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Reaction mechanisms of ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>: 2D contour plot of the <i>in situ</i> XRD within the initial two cycles (a). <i>Ex situ</i> SAED patterns at the different states within initial cycle (bâd). Schematic illustration for reaction mechanism (e).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/asset/images/large/nn1c00131_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.1c00131&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Selected area electron diffraction (SAED) from <i>ex situ</i> TEM was then conducted to further reveal the reaction mechanism complementing the operando study (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>bâd). At the sodiation state of 0.75 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b), the signals of Na<sub>2</sub>S (JCPDS 00-03-0933), Zn (JCPDS 00-001-1238), and Na<sub><i>y</i></sub>âIn<sub>6</sub>S<sub>7</sub> (JCPDS 01-080-0126) from the SAED pattern are detected, suggesting the conversion process and revealing the formation of Zn which is not observed in the <i>in situ</i> XRD results. After being fully sodiated (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c), the generated additional phases are attributed to NaZn<sub>13</sub> (JCPDS No. 00-003-1008) from the alloying process. As charging back to 2.5 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d), the characteristic diffraction pattern of Na<sub><i>b</i></sub>âZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> reappears, affirming the reversible (de)sodiation processes as discussed. Furthermore, we employed the <i>ex situ</i> HRTEM to verify the reversibility of Na<sub><i>b</i></sub>âZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> in the second cycle. Obviously, the <i>d</i><sub>(110)</sub>-spacing of Na<sub><i>b</i></sub>âZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> at the OCV state of second cycle is 0.197 nm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16a</a>). At the sodiation state of 1.1 V in the second cycle (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16b</a>), the <i>d</i><sub>(110)</sub>-spacing of Na<sub><i>b</i></sub>âZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> increases to 0.200 nm, indicating the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup>. As further charging back to 2.5 V (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16c</a>), the <i>d</i><sub>(110)</sub>-spacing reduces back to the 0.197 nm, demonstrating the reversible reaction process and high reversibility of Na<sub><i>b</i></sub>âZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>. In addition, the morphology of ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> retains its pristine state after the initial (de)sodiation cycle (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/suppl_file/nn1c00131_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17</a>), indicating its good structural stability. To summarize, the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> as the SIB anode undergoes a stepwise electrochemical reaction, which is described as follows:<disp-formula-group><figcaption><strong></strong><span class="figure__caption hlFld-FigureCaption"><p>Intercalation reaction</p></span></figcaption><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq2"><div class="equation"><img reaction</p></span></figcaption><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " reaction</p></span></figcaption><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/asset/images/medium/nn1c00131_m005.gif" alt="" id="_i7"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span></disp-formula-group><disp-formula-group><figcaption><strong></strong><span class="figure__caption hlFld-FigureCaption"><p>De-intercalation reaction</p></span></figcaption><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/asset/images/medium/nn1c00131_m006.gif" alt="" id="_i8"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span></disp-formula-group><disp-formula-group><figcaption><strong></strong><span class="figure__caption hlFld-FigureCaption"><p>De-alloying reaction</p></span></figcaption><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/asset/images/medium/nn1c00131_m007.gif" alt="" id="_i9"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span></disp-formula-group><disp-formula-group><figcaption><strong></strong><span class="figure__caption hlFld-FigureCaption"><p>Conversion and de-intercalation reactions</p></span></figcaption><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/asset/images/medium/nn1c00131_m008.gif" alt="" id="_i10"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span></disp-formula-group></div><div class="NLM_p last">The reaction mechanism and corresponding structure evolution can be understood in a more direct manner and are illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e. During the early sodiation process, Na<sup>+</sup> can be easily adsorbed on the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> surface and further intercalated into the interior structure with fast reaction kinetics. This is mainly owing to its open layered feature with large unit-cell volume, as well as the low Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barrier and high Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient. At the subsequent conversion and alloying stages, the Zn-based components usually ensure and deliver large capacity contribution but suffer from poor structural stability. By incorporating indium, the generated indium-based compounds during cycling contribute to a reversible Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation reaction, and hence  induce a complementary working process to relieve the mechanical strain on the electrode during cycling. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In conclusion, ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> as an SIB anode was developed and studied to reveal its sodiation improvement from the conventional ZnS anode. It is revealed that the indium incorporation strategy can not only endow the Zn-based sulfides with a layered structure but also excite a complementary electrochemical process. First, the layered structure induced by the indium incorporation leads to abundant exposed electrochemical active sites and large cell volume of the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, which is beneficial for sodium-ion diffusion and storage to boost its electrochemical kinetics. In addition, the pseudocapacitive behavior of ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> prompts the fast and stable sodium-ion diffusion, and thus benefits its electrochemical kinetics and cycling capabilities. Next, the aroused complementary working process of ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> can ensure the display of high electrochemical capacity without sacrificing its rate and cycling performances, ultimately contributing to its excellent electrochemical performance. In general, these features synergistically endow the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> with highly reversible capacity, excellent rate performance, and stable cycling. As an effective modification strategy and study of electrode materials targeted for high-performance SIBs, this work provides deep insights in the exploration of superior electrode materials for alkali-ion batteries.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i13"> Preparation of ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and ZnS</h3><div class="NLM_p last">For the preparation of ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, 1 mmol zinc chloride (Sigma-Aldrich-14422), 2 mmol indium chloride tetrahydrate (Sigma-Aldrich-334073), and 6 mmol thioacetamide (Sigma-Aldrich-163678) were dispersed in 37.5 mL water with churning for 0.5 h. Then, the solution was put into a Teflon-lined steel autoclave (50 mL) to conduct a reaction at 180 Â°C for 24 h. Next, the products were collected by centrifuging, washing, and freeze-drying. Eventually, the ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> was obtained after a thermal treatment at 500 Â°C for 2 h in Ar flow. Besides, ZnS was synthesized via the same process as described above except without addition of indium chloride tetrahydrate.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i14" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i14"> Structure Characterizations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">XPS (Thermo Fisher Scientific Inc., 250Xi), Raman (WITec, Alpha300), XRD (Bruker, D8 Advance), specific surface area and pore volume analyzer (Autosorb, iQ-MP/XR), TEM (JEOL, JEM-2100F) and SEM (Hitachi, S-4800) were employed to test the chemical environment, composition, phase structure, pore volume, specific surface area, and morphology of the as-prepared samples, respectively.</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i15" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i15"> Electrochemical Characterizations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">For the half cell, electrodes were developed by mixing active materials (80 wt %), Ketjen black (10 wt %; Lion Specialty Chemicals Co., Ltd.-ECP600JD), and sodium alginate (10 wt %; Sigma-Aldrich-W201502) in deionized water to get a slurry and then were cast onto Al foils (MTI Corporation) with mass loading of â1.3 mg cm<sup>â2</sup>. The half batteries were assembled in coin-type cells (MTI Corporation-CR2032) where the electrodes were employed as working electrodes, a piece of sodium (Sigma-Aldrich-483745), as the counter electrodes, glass fiber (Whatman-GF/D), as a separator, and 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME solution, as the electrolyte (DuoDuo-NP-035). For the full battery, a Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C cathode was developed by mixing Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C (80 wt %), Ketjen black (10 wt %; Lion Specialty Chemicals Co., Ltd.-ECP600JD), and polyvinylidene fluoride (10 wt %; Sigma-Aldrich-182702) in <i>N</i>-methyl pyrrolidone solution (Sigma-Aldrich-443778) to form a slurry, which was cast onto Al foils (MTI Corporation) with mass loading of â6.5 mg cm<sup>â2</sup> for cycling test. The anode was prepared via the same process as for the half-cell (mass loading of â1.3 mg cm<sup>â2</sup>). To compensate for the initial irreversible capacity, the anode was presodiated by directly contacting with sodium metal for 60 min. After that, the full batteries were packaged in coin-type cells (MTI Corporation-CR2032) where the as-prepared anodes and cathodes were employed as electrodes, glass fiber (Whatman-GF/D) as separator, and 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in a solution of EC and DMC (volume ratio of 1:1) containing 5.0 wt % fluoroethylene-carbonate as the electrolyte (DuoDuo-NP-007). The calculation of the energy density for the full cell is based on the following equations:<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq3"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c00131/asset/images/medium/nn1c00131_m009.gif" alt="" id="_i16"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span>where <i>P</i> and <i>E</i> mean the power and energy densities, respectively; <i>V</i> is the average discharging voltage (2 V; 50th cycle); <i>i</i>/<i>m</i> is the current density (<i>m</i> is the total mass of the active materials in electrodes; 166.6 mA g<sup>â1</sup>); and <i>t</i> is the discharging time (3180 s; 50th cycle). Electrochemical cycling measurements were tested on Neware battery testing systems. EIS and CV tests were conducted on electrochemical workstations (Bio-Logic, VMP3).</div></div><div id="sec4_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i17" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i17"> Theoretical Simulations (DFT)</h3><div class="NLM_p last">DFT was conducted via VASP, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref66'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref66" aria-label="reference 66">(66)</a> and the ionic potentials were served as the projector augmented wave method. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref67'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref67" aria-label="reference 67">(67)</a> The cutoff energy of 500 eV and a 3 Ã 3 Ã 1 <i>k</i>-point grid were adopted for calculations. The convergence criteria for force (0.02 eV Ã<sup>â1</sup>) and energy (10<sup>â4</sup> eV) were employed, respectively. The exchange-correlation potential was treated through the PerdewâBurkeâErnzerhof function. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref68'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref68" aria-label="reference 68">(68)</a> A vacuum region of 15 Ã was set, and Bader charge analysis was used to calculate the charge transfer between ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/ZnS and Na. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref69'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref69" aria-label="reference 69">(69)</a> Climbing-image nudged elastic band was employed to calculate the Na diffusion barriers on ZnIn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and ZnS. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref70'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref70" aria-label="reference 70">(70)</a></div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div                         cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium/Potassium-Ion Batteries: Boosting the Rate Capability and Cycle Life by Combining Morphology, Defect and Structure Engineering</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Huang, Huijuan; Xu, Rui; Feng, Yuezhan; Zeng, Sifan; Jiang, Yu; Wang, Huijuan; Luo, Wei; Yu, Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">32</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1904320</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADVMEW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0935-9648</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Carbon-based materials have been considered as the most promising anode materials for both sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) and potassium-ion batteries (PIBs), owing to their good chem. stability, high elec. cond., and environmental benignity.  However, due to the large sizes of sodium and potassium ions, it is a great challenge to realize a carbon anode with high reversible capacity, long cycle life, and high rate capability.  Herein, by rational design, N-doped 3D mesoporous carbon nanosheets (N-CNS) are successfully synthesized, which can realize unprecedented electrochem. performance for both SIBs and PIBs.  The N-CNS possess an ultrathin nanosheet structure with hierarchical pores, ultrahigh level of pyridinic N/pyrrolic N, and an expanded interlayer distance.  The beneficial features that can enhance the Na-/K-ion intercalation/deintercalation kinetic process, shorten the diffusion length for both ions and electrons, and accommodate the vol. change are demonstrated.  Hence, the N-CNS-based electrode delivers a high capacity of 239 mAh g-1 at 5 A g-1 after 10 000 cycles for SIBs and 321 mAh g-1 at 5 A g-1 after 5000 cycles for PIBs.  First-principles calcn. shows that the ultrahigh doping level of pyridinic N/pyrrolic N contributes to the enhanced sodium and potassium storage performance by modulating the charge d. distribution on the carbon surface.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpVG-cQvHto_LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhRgqoJQ5vPOQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXpt12kuw%253D%253D&amp;md5=03d6ec4c0f67558dd6c91962115a7d5f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201800492"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ye, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jaroniec, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiao, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The Application of Hollow Structured Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries: From Simple to Complex Systems</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Exploring competitive features of stationary sodium ion batteries for electrochemical energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Tiefeng; Zhang, Yaping; Jiang, Zhanguo; Zeng, Xianqing; Ji, Jiapeng; Li, Zeheng; Gao, Xuehui; Sun, Minghao; Lin, Zhan; Ling, Min; Zheng, Junchao; Liang, Chengdu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Owing to the excellent abundance and availability of sodium reserves, sodium ion batteries (NIBs) show great promise for meeting the material supply and cost demands of large-scale energy storage systems (ESSs) used for the application of renewable energy sources and smart grids.  However, the cost advantages of stationary NIBs alone are not enough to ensure their com. success.  In this review, we summarize the emerging attractive characteristics of stationary NIBs, such as high-rate capability, all-climate operation, and full-battery recyclability.  Together with inherent cost advantages, these merits have resulted in an excellent compatibility between stationary NIBs and large-scale ESSs that dictates their validity in practical applications.  Representative electrode materials are highlighted to illustrate advances in corresponding features.  The insights presented in this review can inspire further research interest into NIB design and serve as a guide for the application of NIBs in large-scale stationary energy storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr7J6sqiEzloLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhtv_SLPk3TtA"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Metal Organic Framework-Templated Synthesis of Bimetallic Selenides with Rich Phase Boundaries for Sodium-Ion Storage and Oxygen Evolution Reaction</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fang, Guozhao; Wang, Qichen; Zhou, Jiang; Lei, Yongpeng; Chen, Zixian; Wang, Ziqing; Pan, Anqiang; Liang, Shuquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Two-phase or multiphase compds. have been evidenced to exhibit good electrochem. performance for energy applications; however, the mechanism insights into these materials, esp. the performance improvement by engineering the high-active phase boundaries in bimetallic compds., remain to be seen.  Here, we report a bimetallic selenide heterostructure (CoSe2/ZnSe) and the fundamental mechanism behind their superior electrochem. performance.  The charge redistribution at the phase boundaries of CoSe2/ZnSe was exptl. and theor. proven.  Benefiting from the abundant phase boundaries, CoSe2/ZnSe exerts low Na+ adsorption energy and fast diffusion kinetics for sodium-ion batteries and high activity for oxygen evolution reaction.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Defect-assisted selective surface phosphorus doping to enhance rate capability of titanium dioxide for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Gan, Qingmeng; He, Hanna; Zhu, Youhuan; Wang, Zhenyu; Qin, Ning; Gu, Shuai; Li, Zhiqiang; Luo, Wen; Lu, Zhouguang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Phosphorus doping is an effective strategy to simultaneously improve the electronic cond. and regulate the ionic diffusion kinetics of TiO2 being considered as anode materials for sodium ion batteries.  However, efficient phosphorus doping at high concn. in well-crystd. TiO2 nanoparticles is still a big challenge.  Herein, the authors propose a defect-assisted phosphorus doping strategy to selectively engineer the surface structure of TiO2 nanoparticles.  The reduced TiO2-x shell layer that is rich in oxygen defects and Ti3+ species precisely triggered a high concn. of phosphorus doping (â¼7.8 at.%), and consequently a TiO2@TiO2-x-P core@shell architecture was produced.  Comprehensive characterizations and first-principle calcns. proved that the surface-functionalized TiO2-x-P thin layer endowed the TiO2@TiO2-x-P with substantially enhanced electronic cond. and accelerated Na ion transportation, resulting in great rate capability (167 mA h g-1 at 10 000 mA g-1) and stable cycling (99% after 5000 cycles at 10 A g-1).  Combining in/ex situ x-ray diffraction with ex situ ESR clearly demonstrated the high reversibility and robust mech. behavior of TiO2@TiO2-x-P upon long-term cycling.  This work provides an interesting and effective strategy for precise heteroatoms doping to improve the electrochem. performance of nanoparticles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoV2X3aWTMl9bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhtv_SLPk3TtA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXhsVamtb7P&amp;md5=cd8e232f3259e7881601dbcf23b2e6ee</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201800212"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Exploration of Advanced Electrode Materials for Rechargeable Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Facile Synthesis of Hierarchical Hollow CoP@C Composites with Superior Performance for Sodium and Potassium Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Qiannan; Hu, Zhe; Liang, Yaru; Li, Lin; Zou, Chao; Jin, Huile; Wang, Shun; Lu, Huanming; Gu, Qinfen; Chou, Shu-Lei; Liu, Yong; Dou, Shi-Xue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
